discoverers: ['Viscara', 'Hill', 'Tattersall', 'Innes']
BODY:
1901 1(1901la). 
Bright southern comet. 
Discovered by Viscara (Uruguay) 
on 12 April and independently by Hill (Queenstown, Africa) and Tattersall 
(Australia) on 23 April. Appeared as a bright 1 to 2™ object with a noticeable 
$51 


tail in the bright morning sky. 
In April, moved eastward between Pisces 
and Cetus; after the perihelion passage (24 April), observed in the evening 
sky. 
In May, moved between Eridanus and Taurus; 
in the middle of May, 
crossed Orion: 
at the end of May and in June, passed through Monoceros 
and Canis Minor, receding from the Sun and the Earth. 
24 April, nucleus visible for some time after the sunrise. 
Innes (Cape) 
described the comet as bright (0 to 1™) with a 10° tail curving south; 
deep 
yellow comet; nucleus equal in brightness to Mercury. 
Nucleus exceeded 
Sirius in brightness (-1™.58). 
3 May, a secondary straight tail was reported; 
6 May, secondary tail 30° long extending at 40° to the main tail, which was 
1/4 of the secondary tail's length. 
One of the photographs taken on 6 May 
showed two additional rays between the main and the straight tails. 
5 May, 
Nijland(Sumatra) described the comet as brighter than Aldebaran. 
5.3 May, 
intrinsic brightness 1™.0; 
6.3 May, 2™.3. 
At that time, 
the nucleus was 
estimated at 3 to 3.5. 
12 May, nucleus 5™.4; 
18 May, 6™.2. 
7 May, Hill 
reported a 16"' nucleus. 
15 May, the nucleus split into two components 
separated by 1" and differing by 1™ in brightness. 
18 May, only one nucleus 
observed. 
27 May, tail 50' long; 2 July to 6 July, 
D=11/2 to 1'; nucleus 
OP onto Lomas 
Accordingto Nijland, 
who collected the estimates of sailors observing 
the comet at sea, the main tail was 15° long on 14 May to 18 May; the 
straight, faint tail was occasionally tracked over 3 times this distance. 
The tails were also observed by Lunt (South Africa), Otten (Peru), Prosé 
(Brazil), 
et al. 
The main tail was divided along the axis by a dark gap, the 
"shadow" of the nucleus. 
5 May and 8 May, the angle between the main and 
the secondary tail was 15°; 15 May, 
11°. According to Hill, the nucleus 
had a continuous spectrum with some bright lines. 
Comet photographed by 
Hill (Cape of Good Hope) and Barnard (Yerkes Obs.). 
Important drawings 
made by Nijland(Sumatra), Innes (Cape), Prose (Brazil), Viscara (Uruguay), 
and others. 
From 23 May, comet was invisible to the naked eye. 
Measured last on 
13 June by Tebbutt (Windsor, 8-inch refractor) and on 14 June by Innes 
(Cape, 7-inch refractor) (10 to 11™). 
Retemences eA. Ni tooo) Seas Ns. U6 soos A Jeol, lees 
cs 
Orbit: Merfield. —A.Jb., 5:188; Astr. Abh., 4: (2). 
V II gave, according to the estimates of Nijland, Aitken, Innes et al., 
Hip =5™.9,. 
From the estimates for the beginning of May, Hi)=4™.0. 
In June, 
m=10 to 11™; hence Hjg=7™.2. 
2 June, D,=1'.8. 
12 May and 19 May, the 
main tail 
S=0.15 and 0.26a.u. respectively; the straight narrow tail 
S=0.54 and 0.48 a. u. 
F.A, Bredikhin, 
Bull. Acad. Petersburg, 15(5): 451; A.N., 158: 219, 
proceeding from photographs and drawings, classified the main tail as 
type II and the isolated straight rays as type II synchron and type III tail; 
trace of a type I tail. 
N.D. Moiseev according to the suggestion of S. V. Orlov, 
who considered F.A. Bredikhin's conclusions erroneous, 
reconsidered the 
nature of the main tail, showing that it could be a type III synchron produced 
by the explosion on 26 May.-A. Zh., 2: 73. 1925. 
Unfortunately the entire 
available body of data was not analyzed, and therefore the opinion of 
S.V. Orlov and N.D. Moiseev requires additional confirmation (S. V. Orlov 
in his book ''Komety" gives only a type III tail for comet 1901 I). The comet 
352 


undoubtedly had a type I tail, apparently type II synchrons and possibly a 
type III tail. 
On the spectrum of the comet cf. Hill —M.N., 61: 508.

0.88); 24 May —(0.89, 1.48);

1901 II (1901b). 
The twenty ninth recorded apparition 
of Comet Encke. 
Spotted near Thonberg's ephemeris position by Wilson (Northsfield) on the 
morning of 5 Aug. Located in Auriga, moved through Gemini (August), 
Cancer (end of August) and Leo (beginning of September) and then passed 
rapidly to Ophiuchus (October). 
8 Aug., observers in Konigsberg and Heidelberg reported a very diffuse 
comet lacking any sharp nucleus, D=1'; 9 Aug., central condensation, 
.

11 Aug., nucleus more obvious.

19 Aug., nebula 
with D=1'.5, as bright as an 8.0 comparison star. According to Holetschek, 
18 Aug., m=8™,1;

in bright twilight, 
6™.5.

brightness from 9™.5 to 8™, D=1'. 
Brightness estimates were also made 
in Strasbourg and Bamberg. 
1 Sept., Cerulli (Teramo) reported traces of 
a tail at 
P=250°.

At the beginning of October, Tebbutt (Windsor) failed to detect the comet 
in evening twilight (fainter than 8 to 9™),

### ENTRY 003 | 1902 I (1902a) | book p.353 pdf p.172 | pdes=1902 G1 | basis=name (Brooks) + year | conf=high
discoverers: ['Brooks', 'Hartwig', 'Aitken', 'Wolf']
BODY:
1902 I (1902a). 
Discovered in Pegasus by Brooks (Geneva, N.Y.) on the 
morning of 14 April as a bright telescopic object (7 to 8™) with a small star- 
like nucleus and a short narrow tail. 
Moved south-east to conjunction with 
the Sun. 
16 April, Hartwig (Bamberg) estimated m = 8™.5; circular comet witha 
9™ condensation, D=3', tail 25' in the direction 
P=300°. 
16 April, Aitken 
(Lick Obs.) gave m =8™,5, straight tail extending over 30! away from the 
Sun; a condensation without any starlike nucleus observed in the head. 
17 April and 18 April, observed in Pola; faint at dawn with a 10 to 10™.5 
nucleus. 
18 April, photographs (7-minute exposure) taken by Wolf 
(Heidelberg) showed a wide faint tail; the southern, concave edge of the 
tail was noticeably brighter than the northern edge. Observed also in 
Copenhagen and Arcetri. 
After 19 April, invisible in morning glow; in May, 
after the perihelion passage, observers at the Lick Observatory searched 
for the comet in vain with 12- and 36-inch refractors. 
References: A.N., 158, 159; M.N., 62, 63; V.J.S., 38. 
Orbit: Kreutz and Stromgren. —V.J.S., 1903. 65 (not certain). 
From V.I1., Hyg=11™.7; hy =12™.2. 
D,=1'.5; 
S=0.004. 
The shape of the 
tail indicates type II or type III. 
(r, A): 14 April —(0.73, 0.51); 20 April —(0.63, 0.52),

### ENTRY 004 | 1902 II (1902c) | book p.354 pdf p.173 | pdes=26P | basis=name (Grigg) + year | conf=high
discoverers: ['Grigg']
BODY:
1902 II (1902c). 
The first recorded apparition of the short-period 
Comet Grigg-Skjellerup. 
Discovered on 22 July by Grigg (Thames, New 
Zealand) with a 31/2-inch telescope. 
Located between Leo and Virgo, 
moving south-east; 
at the beginning of August, located in Virgo. 
Because 
of a delay in the discovery report, observed only by Grigg up to 3 Aug., 
when it was very faint in haze (9 to 10™). According to his descriptions, 
the comet appeared as a faint nebula 1'.5 in diameter. 
Could not have been 
fainter than 9 to 10™, since observed and measured witha 31/2-inchtelescope. 
References: A.N., 159; M.N., 63; V.J.S., 38. 
Orbit: Merton. —Mem. R.A.S., 64: 74. 
In V. Il, Hj =9™. 
Estimating m=8 to 10™for 22 Aug. to 29 Aug., we 
obtain Hjp=9 to 10™, 
D,=1'.6. 
(r, A): 23 July —(0.90, 1.10); 4 Aug. —(1.00, ite)

### ENTRY 005 | 1902 III (1902b) | book p.354 pdf p.173 | pdes=1902 R1 | basis=name (Perrine) + year | conf=high
discoverers: ['Perrine', 'Borrelly', 'Perseus', 'Ophiuchus', 'Holetschek', 'Nijland', 'Wirtz', 'Barnard']
BODY:
1902 III (1902b). 
Discovered in Perseus by Perrine (Lick Obs.) with a 
12-inch refractor on the night between 31 Aug. and1Sept. asa 9™ object, D=4 
to5', slightly elongated with a well-developed 10.5 to 11™ nucleus and a short 
(8 to 10') wide tail (jet?) extending south-west. 
Discovered independently 
in Europe by Borrelly (Marseilles) on 2 Sept. Moved through Perseus 
(September) Cassiopeia (end of September), Lacerta, Cygnus (beginning of 
October), Lyra, Hercules and Ophiuchus (October-November); 
in December, 
descended south, passing through Scorpio, Libra and Centaurus. 
In 
January 1903 passed along Centaurus and Vela; in February, crossed 
Puppis and Canis Major; in March, 
moved through Monoceros, ascending north. 
Extensive observations of appearance and brightness were carried out, 
particularly by Holetschek (Vienna), Nijland (Utrecht), Wirtz (Strasbourg), 
Perrine (Lick Obs.) Barnard (Yerkes Obs.) and observers at Lyon and 
Copenhagen. 
According to Holetschek, 
3 Sept. to 5 Sept., m=8™.5, D=2', nucleus 
9™,5; 20 Sept. and 21 Sept., 
m=7™.5, D=3', tail 30'; 27 Sept. 
to 30 Sept., 
m =5.5 to 47,9, D=4!', tail 15'; 8 Oct. to 15 Oct., maximum apparent 
brightness (4.2 to 4™.1), D=4', tail 30 to 60' (visual observations), nucleus 
9™_5; at the beginning of November, m =4™.5 to 57.5, D=3 to 1.5, tail 20! 
long. Nijlandestimated the intrinsic brightness at 3™.8 and the tail length at60'. 
19 Sept. to29Oct., observed by Modestov (Moscow, witha 15-inchrefractor; 
at the beginning of September, observed by Sokoloff (Pulkovo). On 27 nights 
between 6 Sept. and 29 Oct., the comet was photographed with the 30-inch 
reflector of the Greenwich Observatory. 
26 Sept. to 29 Sept., long-exposure 
plates showed a bright circular head, no less than 7 rays and a long 1° tail 
directed approximately away from the Sun. 
Photographs taken on 25 Sept., 
26 Sept., 
7 Oct., 
8 Oct. and 9 Oct., by Sikora (Yur'ev) with 2 to 4 1/2 
hours exposure time showed two tails; the long straight tail extended over 
3° and the second short tail was pear-shaped; 
the long tail was classified 
by Sikora as type I, the short one as type III. 
25 Sept. and 1 Oct., photo- 
graphed at the Meudon and Harvard Observatories, and also by Roberts 
and M. Wolf, the latter trying to detect the stereoscopic effect on photo- 
graphs of the comet. 
Extensive photographic observations were carried out by Curtis (Lick 
Obs.) with 78 and 83-cm cameras and exposure times from 25 minutes to 
6 hours. In the second half of October photographs showed an 11° straight 
tail; the secondary tail was highly curved and considerably shorter than 
354 


the main tail but nearly equally bright. 
Photographs show numerous rays 
and considerable activity in the tail; the nature of the ray systems changed 
radically within 24 hours. 
17 Nov., observed for the last time at northern observatories before 
disappearing in the solar rays. 
After the perihelion passage, observed in 
Santiago from 11 Dec. 1902 to 4 Jan. 1903. 
Tebbutt (Windsor) observed the 
comet up to 16 Feb.; Wiggin (Cordoba), up to 5 March 1903, 
The comet 
was observed from 29 Jan. 1903 at Lick and University Park Observatories; 
in February, 
11 to 12™. Wirtz (Strasbourg) described the comet asa circular 
diffuse nebula with slight condensation. 
23 Feb. to 28 Feb., Aitken (Lick 
Obs.) reported very faint irregular comet, no nucleus. 
Measured last by 
Howe (University Park) on 30 March; 
17 April not spotted with a 36-inch 
refractor. 
References: A.N., 159, 160; M.N., 63; B.A., 21; A.J., 41; L.O.B., 
28: 42; V.J.S., 38, 39. 
Orbit: Peck and Lindsey. —A.J., 27. Remarkably close encounter with 
Mercury (29 Nov.) at a distance of 0.023 a. u. 
In V.I, according to Holetschek's observations, y=11.5; Hj=6™.0. 
The 
same source gave, according to the estimates of Nijland, Graff, Wirtz et al., 
y= 9:2; Hp =5™.7; in .GsA.M. ,. Hip = 6™.0. 
Bobrovnikoff, Perkins Obs. Contr. 
15. 1941 and 16. 1942, used 89 esti- 
mates of various observers (Holetschek and Nijland included) to obtain 
y =6.6 and Hy =6™.8; these parameters gave too low values for brightness 
on 8 Oct. and 9 Oct. (5™.2 instead of 3.8 to 4™.0); for the end of March 1903 
they yielded 
m =10™.6, which bears no resemblance to the actual values. 
D, = 3' (according to visual observations for 20 Oct. to 25 Oct.) and 
D,=11' (photographs taken at the beginning of October). 
Smax=0.12 (end of 
October; photographed by Curtis). 
The long tail of the comet was undoubtedly 
of type I; the second tail was apparently of type III. 
Spectrum photographed by de la Baume Pluvinel, C.R., 136: 143, and 
studied by Miller, Science, 16: 868. Almost no continuous spectrum whatso- 
ever; common bands observed.

### ENTRY 008 | 1903 III (1903b) | book p.357 pdf p.176 | pdes=1903 H1 | basis=name (Grigg) + year | conf=high
discoverers: ['Grigg']
BODY:
1903 Ill (1903b). 
Discovered in Eridanus by Grigg (New Zealand) on the 
evening of 17 April. Moved north-east (away from the Sun) through Eridanus 
(beginning of May), Lepus and Canis Major (end of May). 
Observed only in New Zealand, Australia and atthe Cape. Upon discovery, 
appeared faint through a 3-inch telescope; no nucleus (fainter than 8 to 9™). 
Receded from the Sun (perihelion passage on 26 March) growing rapidly 
faint. 
Measured last on 28 May in Windsor with an 8-inch refractor and 
at the Cape of Good Hope with a 7-inch refractor when hardly visible (not 
fainter than 12 to 13™). 
References: M.N., 64; V.J.S., 39. 
Orbit: Peck. —A.J.; 24:51:75: 
In V.II estimated onthe assumption that m = 13™.5 (obvious underestimate). 
From the estimates above we obtain Hjg = 8™.6 to 9™.6. 
(r, 4): 17 April —(0.80, 1.50); 
5 May —(1.04, 1.41); 29 May —(1.43, 1.65).

1903 IV (1903c). Discovered in Aquarius by Borrelly (Marseilles) on 
21 June. 
Later, 
detected on plates taken at the Harvard Obs. on 28 May 
and 30 May. Upon discovery, fairly bright (8™?) with a 10™ nucleus anda 
10' fan-shaped tail; brightness rapidly increased. 
Moved north-west 
through Pegasus (end of June), Cygnus (middle of July), Draco and Ursa 
Major (end of July and August); after disappearance in the solar rays, ob- 
served in Hydra and Antlia (end of September and October); 
in July and 
August, very favorable position in the northern sky made possible numerous 
visual and photographic observations.

Curtis and Albrecht (Lick Obs.) photographed the comet with 83-cm and

Changes were observed 
in the straight tail;

In July and August, the long tail was traced 
over 10°, changing in shape from day to day; it was directed precisely 
away from the Sun and split into two distinct branches at a 7° angle to one 
another. 
In August, numerous 
condensations 
were 
observed in the tail. 
20 June, the secondary (curved) tail formed a 22° angle with the main tail; 
in the middle of July, both tails coincided; 
18 Aug., the angle between the 
two tails was 33°; numerous rays observed, 
Barnard (Yerkes Obs.) photographed the comet with a small camera, 
reporting a 17° tail on several nights. 
24 July, sharp change in tail's 
shape; part of the tail seemed to separate from the main body; 23 July and 
25 July, no anomalies reported; "separation" of the tail registered on 
plates taken by Quénisset, Roberts and Smith (Yale Obs.). 
After the perihelion passage, measured at the Cape Obs. from 30 Sept. 
to 22 Oct., growing rapidly faint; on the last days, not fainter than 11 to Wau.

BODY:
1907 IV (1907d). 
Discovered by Daniel (Princeton, 
U. S.A.) on 9 June 
near the vernal equinox as a nebula of m=9™.5 and D=2! with a 10™ conden- 
sation. 
Moving north-west, passed through Pisces (June-July), Aries, 
Taurus (July), Gemini Cancer (August) and Leo (September). 
From 24 Sept., 
disappeared in the solar rays. 
At the beginning of November, located in 
Virgo; then moved in Libra (January to March 1908); from April to June, 
again located in Virgo. 
Along with 1908 III, comet 1907 IV yielded many new observational data 
which facilitated the explanation of the specific physical processes in 
comets. 
Numerous photometric, photographic and spectral observations 
were obtained, 
the most significant being the estimates of intrinsic and 
nuclear brightness due to Holetschek (Vienna), Nijland (Utrecht) Wirtz 
(Strasbourg), Schwab (Kremsmunster) et al. 
Already by 20 June, 
m=8™, D=6 
to 7'; a tail pointing west. 
1 July to 
5 July, 
m=7™6; 
10 July, 4™.2; 21 July to 31 July, 37.2, D=5 to 9', visual 
observations gave a tail 2 to 3° long; 2 Aug. to 9 Aug., 2.8 to 27.5; 10Aug. 
to 19 Aug., 2.1 to 2™,3, tail 6 to 9°; 20 Aug. and 30Aug., 2™, D=2', tail ~2°; 
inSeptember, nucleus 4 to 5™ in twilight. 
12 Aug. to9Sept., measured at the 
Catania Obs. witha wedge photometer; brightness deviatedfrom the Po As daw. 
Comet extensively photographed on 36 nights between 10 June and 12 Sept. 
by Duncan (Lick Obs.) (71 plates); 21 July to 27 Aug., Wolf (Heidelberg) 
obtained 41 negatives within 10 nights; 
12 Aug. to 11 Sept., G.A. Tikhov 
(Pulkovo) obtained 11 photographs on the Bredikhin astrograph (with filters); 
11 July to 8 Sept., photographed by Barnard (Yerkes Obs.) (25 negatives); 
12 July to 25 July, photographed by Quenisset (Juvisi), 
I. and J. Fri¢ and 
Nusl (Ondrejov Obs.), Riccd, Horn, Bemporad (Catania Obs.) and others. 
10 July to 12 July, main tail appeared as a straight, homogeneous band; 
a system of jet-rays undergoing rapid changes emerged from the head 
(Barnard observed changes take place within 2 to 6 hours). 
Photographs 
taken at the Lick Obs. showed tail condensations. 
In the second half of July 
and in August, the tail consisted of a multitude of rays emerging from the 
head. 
Duncan reported in August a dark stripe extending from the nucleus 
along the middle of the tail (''shadow" of the nucleus); tail up to 17° long. 
In September, 
a parabolic envelope with dark axial gap. 
Ray structures 
located outside the orbital plane, 
a conclusion drawn from the appear- 
ance of the comet when the Earth passed through its orbital plane on 
15 July. 
Considering 59 photographs between 10 July and 27 Aug., Horn 
* This example illustrates the preferability of medium power instruments for observing diffuse nebulosities.

deduced that the axis of the tail cone oscillated with a period of 16 hours, 
deviating from the orbital plane. A detailed study of the photographs was 
undertaken by Kopff, who attempted to determine solar repulsive forces and 
to investigate the rays and the jets. Polaroscopic observations of Duncan 
(8 Aug. to 12 Aug.) indicated no reflected sunlight in the tail.

BODY:
1908 III (1908c). 
Discovered at the Des Moines (Iowa) University 
Observatory on 1 Sept. by Morehouse on a photograph showing the comet 
as a 9™ object with a long tail. 
Discovered independently by Borrelly 
(Marseilles) on 3 Sept. 
One of the remarkable comets of the 20th century that played a particular 
role in the evolution of our knowledge of the processes in cometary heads 
and tails. 
Located in Camelopardus; moved through Cassiopeia, Cepheus 
(September), Cygnus, Lyra (October), Hercules, Aquila (November), 
Scutum and Sagittarius (December) approaching conjunction with the Sun. 
At the northern observatories, observed till 14 Dec. (Rome and Lick Obs.); 
in the southern hemisphere, observed from 22 Jan. 1909. 
Passed through 
Corona Australis, Telescopium (February 1909), Pavo, Apus, Chamaeleon 
(March), Carina, Vela (April) and Pyxis (May). 
Favorable celestial position and considerable brightness of the comet 
enabled numerous observers to obtain prolonged series of photometric, 
photographic and spectrographic observations. 
The most important were 
the estimates of intrinsic and nuclear brightness and size of head and tail 
undertaken by Holetschek (Vienna) 13 Sept. to 5 Dec., Nijland (Utrecht, 
375 


14 Sept. to 19 Nov.), Wirtz (Strasbourg),

(Uccle), 30 Sept. to 11 Dec., Abetti (Arcetri), 28 Sept. to 27 Oct., and 
Buttner (Breslau), 
13 Oct. to 13 Dec. 
Plate XXXI, Comet 1908 III (Morehouse). Photographed by S. K. Kostinskii on 
23 Oct., exposure 2 hours

m =5™,4 to 57.8; 30 Jan. 1909, Prager gave m=6™.3; silvery-white comet. 
Remarkable photographs were taken by Barnard (Yerkes Obs. ), and many others. 
Numerous photographs were taken in February and March 1909 by Curtis 
in Santiago (Lick Obs. expedition). The photographs revealed the complicated 
ray structure of the head and the tail, and rapid changes occurring in the 
376 


envelopes and the tail.

densations occurred on 16, 29 to 30 Sept.; 
1 Oct. 
to 2 Oct.;

7 Oct.; 
15 Oct. to 17 Oct. 
At the end of September,

long; 
in October, 
9 to 11°; in November, 
8 to 11°. 
Observers, 
particularly 
Barnard and Wolf, studied the numerous instances of high activity of the 
comet and interesting cases of ''waves'"' in the tail. Enormous accelerations 
were determined by Eddington who studied the motion of the envelopes and 
the rays. 
Measuredfirst on 10 May 1909 by Ristenpart (Santiago) witha 24-cm refractor.

1910 1(1910a). 
Brilliant comet first spotted on 12 Jan. by diamond 
miners in Transvaal; 
15 Jan., detected by railway workers at Copier 
Junction in the Republic of Orange; Innes (Johannesburg), 
after having 
read the newspaper report, observed the comet on 17 Jan. (16.8 hours 
universal time). 
Exceptionally bright comet observed in daytime only 4° 
from the Sun. 
18 Jan., measured in Vienna, 
Algiers and Rome; 
19 Jan., 
in Cambridge and Santiago; beginning 20Jan., observed throughout the world; 
20 Jan. and 21 Jan., discovered independently by various amateurs; 
equal 
in brightness to Venus, 
a 10° tail. 
Upon discovery, located in Sagittarius, 
moving towards the Sun and then north-east through Aquarius and Pegasus 
(February), rapidly receding from the Earth and the Sun. 
In March, April, 
and May, slowly crossed Pegasus, moving north; 
in June, moved to Cygnus; 
in July, located above w Cygni. 
17 Jan., D=5', tail 1° long; after the perihelion passage (17 Jan.) tail 
developed rapidly, becoming more than 40° long at the end of January. 
18 Jan., Wright described the comet as several times brighter than Venus 
at its maximum brightness.

and Juvisy on 9 Oct. to 15 Oct., showed a straight main tail 5°.5 long (P = 79°).

10 to 15' long, curving; 
invisible to the naked eye; main tail traced visually over only 1°; m =4™.4; 
D=3'; bluish comet.

* This makes obvious the absolute reliability of photometric observations of comets when due allowance is 
made for the systematic effects characteristic of the methods used. A purely formal approach to photo- 
metric cometary data, such as the use of fictitious dependencies on the objective aperture of the instrument 
without taking into account the structure of the comet (nucleus, head), as practiced by Bobrovnikoff, leads, 
on the contrary, to totally erroneous results.

1914 V (1913f). 
Discovered 10 months before the perihelion passage by

and Lynx;

made the comet visible throughout the world.

1921 II (1921a).

south; after conjunction with the Sun, moved in Orion and Lepus. 
Because of exceptionally favorable position in the sky and the consider-

1925 II (1927j). 
The short-period Comet Schwassmann-Wachmann (1). 
Discovered photographically by Schwassmann and Wachmann (Bergedorf) 
on 15 Nov. 1927 as a circular 13 to 14™ nebula with D=2' and a distinct 
nucleus. 
At opposition located in Pisces, moving extremely slowly south- 
west. 
It was later established that the orbit was almost circular, 
slightly 
inclined to the ecliptic and situated between the orbits of Jupiter and Saturn. 
Therefore, 
the comet, like a planet, can be observed at all points of its 
orbit, with oppositions every 13 months;.-however, 
the comet is usually 
accessible to large instruments only. 
23 Nov., Castro (Santiago) gave m=12™ through a 28-cm refractor, D=2'. 
The most conclusive and continuous brightness estimates and general ob- 
servations were due to van Biesbroeck: 
26 Nov., m=14™5, nucleus Lem,

incidentally, 
this seems to be a specific feature of 
all comets which move at considerable distances from theSun. InJanuary and

December 1928, m=i6".5. 
6 Nov. 1929, Baade described the comet as 
fainter than 17™, but already on 2 Dec. he gave m=13™.5.

would increase to 2 to 0™ 
D,=10' (external envelope) and 4 to 5! (normal

Brightness fluctuations of Comet Schwassmann-Wachmann 1 were studied 
by Richter, A.N., 271: 207. 
He tried to correlate them with solar activity 
and fluctuations in the ultraviolet radiation; 
later, he associated them with the 
solar corpuscular streams which give rise to magnetic disturbances, ASSNES 
277: 12 and 280. 
These streams (corona rays) should be held responsible 
for the brightness flares of comets at substantial distances from the Sun.

### ENTRY 105 | 1925 XI (1925d) | book p.438 pdf p.257 | pdes=1925 V1 | basis=name (Peltier) + year | conf=high
discoverers: ['Peltier', 'Wilk', 'Biesbroeck', 'Chofardet']
BODY:
1925 XI (1925d). 
First discovered by Peltier (Delphos, U.S.A.) on 
13 Nov. as a bright 8™ nebula located above v Bootis; however, according 
to the same report, 
it was not detected on 16 Nov. either at the Harvard or 
the Yerkes Observatories. 
19 Nov., discovered by Wilk (Krakéw[Cracow]) in 
Hercules (8™.5). Afterwards identified on Harvard plates of 16 Nov. and 
17 Nov. 
and on an 18 Nov. plate taken in Babelsberg. 
Moved very rapidly 
south-east; 
18 Nov., encounter with the Earth, 4=0.58a.u.; passed through 
Hercules, Aquila, Capricornus and disappeared in the solar rays at the end 
of December; 
after conjunction, 
at the end of March should have been located 
in Microscopium. 
In November and December, observed visually and photographically at 
numerous observatories. 
22 Nov., van Biesbroeck (Yerkes Obs.) described 
the comet as a diffuse 7”.8 nebula with D=2' and a large nucleus; plates 
showed a faint narrow tail 15' longs 28 Nov., m=%7™.3 in moonlight; 
2 Dec., 
8™,2; 
18 Dec., circular nebula of 9.5; 26 Dec., 
10™ at low altitude; 
29 Dec., 
10™.5; 
31 Dec., quite diffuse when 5° above the horizon. 
26 Nov., 
Chofardet (Besancon) gave 
m= 7™, D=3!' in moonlight; 
2 Dec., 
6™.7; 6 Dec., 
7 to 8™; 
18 Dec., 
10™ when near the horizon. 
Observed at Vienna, Munich, Stockholm, 
Paris, Kazan', Strasbourg, 
Tashkent, Dusseldorf, 
Sonneberg, 
Heidelberg, Cracow, Frankfurt and 
other places. 
Photographs taken at the Yerkes Obs. on 10 Dec. (after waning of the 
moon) showed a 1° tail D=3', 
at P=53°; 
a wide stream 
of rays extended 
from the head; 
at 20', a curve 
(condensation?). 
8 Dec., Wolf's plates 
showed a glow extending south over 10 to 17'; the southern edge of the tail 
was clearer than the northern over almost the entire length. 
No data on 
observations in the southern sky.
